--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (C)**
Reasoning:
**(A) Yes, against Telco but not Rhodes.**
*   **Telco:** Telco negligently allowed the pole to become termite-ridden, creating a dangerous condition. The weakened pole *was* a substantial factor in Walker’s injury when it fell a week later, and the fall was a natural and probable consequence of the termite damage combined with the initial impact. Thus, Telco meets the elements of negligence and proximate cause.
*   **Rhodes:** Rhodes was intoxicated and speeding when she hit the pole. Her actions were *also* a substantial factor in the pole falling (the initial impact weakened it further). It is foreseeable that hitting a telephone pole with a car, especially while intoxicated and speeding, could cause it to fall and injure someone. Thus, Rhodes meets the elements of negligence and proximate cause.
    This choice is incorrect because it absolves Rhodes of liability when her actions contributed to the harm.

**(B) Yes, against Rhodes but not Telco.**
*   **Rhodes:** As explained above, Rhodes meets the elements of negligence and proximate cause.
*   **Telco:** The termite damage was a substantial factor in the pole falling. Even if Rhodes hadn’t hit the pole, it was already weakened and likely to fall eventually. This meets the elements of negligence and proximate cause.
    This choice is incorrect because it absolves Telco of liability when its negligence contributed to the harm.

**(C) Yes, against Telco and Rhodes, each for one-half of his damages.**
*   **Telco:** As explained above, Telco meets the elements of negligence and proximate cause.
*   **Rhodes:** As explained above, Rhodes meets the elements of negligence and proximate cause.
*   Both Telco and Rhodes contributed to the harm. Telco’s negligence created the weakened condition, and Rhodes’s negligence caused the initial impact that led to the pole falling. The damage is apportionable between the two. This choice correctly identifies both parties as liable.

**(D) Yes, against both Telco and Rhodes for the full amount of his damages.**
*   **Telco:** As explained above, Telco meets the elements of negligence and proximate cause.
*   **Rhodes:** As explained above, Rhodes meets the elements of negligence and proximate cause.
*   While both are liable, joint and several liability isn’t established by the facts. The damage is more appropriately apportioned. This choice incorrectly assigns full liability to both parties.

Classifying: Sand Company operated an installation for distributing sand and gravel. The installation was adjacent to a residential area. On Sand's grounds there was a chute with polished metal sides for loading sand and gravel into trucks. The trucks being loaded stopped on the public street below the chute. After closing hours, a plywood screen was placed in the chute and the ladder used for inspection was removed to another section of the installation. For several months, however, a number of children, 8 to 10 years of age, had been playing on Sand's property and the adjoining street after closing hours. The children found the ladder and also discovered that they could remove the plywood screen from the chute and slide down to the street below. Sand knew of this activity. One evening, the children were using the chute as a play device. As an automobile driven by Commuter approached the chute, Ladd, an 8-year-old boy, slid down just in front of the automobile. Commuter applied her brakes, but they suddenly failed, and she hit and injured Ladd. Commuter saw the child in time to have avoided hitting him if her brakes had worked properly. Two days previously, Commuter had taken her car to Garage to have her brakes inspected. Garage inspected the brakes and told her that the brakes were in perfect working order. Claims were asserted on behalf of Ladd by his proper legal representative against Sand, Commuter, and Garage. On Ladd's claim against Garage, will Ladd prevail?

Answer choices:
  (A) Yes, because Garage is strictly liable in tort.
  (B) Yes, if Garage was negligent in inspecting Commuter's brakes.
  (C) No, if Ladd was in the legal category of a bystander.
  (D) No, because Sand 's conduct was an independent and superseding cause.
Classification: multi_hop — This question requires analyzing negligence, duty of care (Garage's inspection), and potential intervening/superseding causes (Sand's knowledge of children playing and Commuter's brake failure) to determine if Ladd can prevail against Garage.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (B)**
Reasoning:
(A) Yes, because Garage is strictly liable in tort. - The research does not support strict liability for a mechanic’s inspection. Source 5 discusses strict liability for *manufacture defects*, which is not applicable here. This choice is missing the element of a valid cause of action (negligence).
(B) Yes, if Garage was negligent in inspecting Commuter's brakes. - This choice correctly identifies the necessary element for Garage to be liable: negligence. If Garage was negligent in inspecting the brakes (failing to detect a problem that existed at the time of inspection), then that negligence is a potential cause in fact of Ladd’s injuries. The research states that a defendant is liable for injuries proximately caused by their negligence. This choice acknowledges the need for negligence, which is the basis for potential liability.
(C) No, if Ladd was in the legal category of a bystander. - The research does not address the legal status of bystanders. This choice introduces an irrelevant factor. It does not address the core issue of Garage’s potential negligence and its causal link to the injury.
(D) No, because Sand 's conduct was an independent and superseding cause. - While Sand’s conduct is relevant to the overall situation, the question specifically asks about liability of *Garage*. The research does not define “independent and superseding cause” and does not apply it to this scenario. Even if Sand’s conduct were a superseding cause, it wouldn’t absolve Garage of liability if Garage was initially negligent. This choice attempts to shift focus away from Garage’s potential negligence.

"The judgment of that federal court shall be transmitted to the head of the federal agency dispensing such funds who, if satisfied that the judgment is fair and lawful, shall execute the judgment according to its terms." This law is

Answer choices:
  (A) constitutional, because disagreements over federal grant-in-aid funds necessarily involve federal questions within the judicial power of the United States.
  (B) constitutional, because the spending of federal monies necessarily includes the authority to provide for the effective settlement of disputes involving them.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (A)**
Reasoning:
**(A) Vendor's telegram received on June 2 was merely a price quotation, not an offer.** This is the best defense. The initial telegram from Bye Bye was an inquiry ("At what price…"). Vendor’s response stated “You’re in luck. We have only 100 QT’s…at 50% off…” This language does not demonstrate a present intent to contract. It merely states a price and quantity available, which is considered a price quotation, not an offer. An offer must demonstrate present intent to enter a binding agreement.

**(B) Bye Bye's letter received on June 5 was not an acceptance because it varied the terms of Vendor's initial telegram.** This is incorrect. Even if the June 2 telegram *was* an offer, Bye Bye’s June 5 letter stating “I accept. Would prefer to pay in full 30 days after invoice” is a valid acceptance *with* a proposed modification (payment terms). Under the UCC 2-207, additional terms in an acceptance generally do not invalidate the acceptance unless acceptance is expressly conditional on assent to the additional terms. Here, Bye Bye clearly accepted, and the payment term is an additional term, not a condition.

**(C) Bye Bye's use of the mails in response to Vendor's initial telegram was an ineffective method of acceptance.** This is incorrect. The mailbox rule generally applies, and acceptance is effective upon dispatch. The method of acceptance (mail) is reasonable in this context.

**(D) Bye Bye's letter received on June 9 was an unequivocal refusal to perform that excused Vendor even if the parties had previously formed a contract.** This is incorrect. The June 9 letter ("I don't deal with people…") is a negative statement, but it doesn’t necessarily constitute a clear refusal to perform. More importantly, the question hinges on whether a contract was formed *before* this letter, and the initial communication likely did not create a contract. Furthermore, the telegram on June 10 shows a willingness to perform on Vendor’s terms, negating any prior refusal.

Hard step cap (3 completed). Routing to VERIFY_ANSWER.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (D)**
Reasoning:
**(A) No, because Prad was a trespasser.** - This is incorrect. While Prad went behind a counter with a "No Admittance, Employees Only" sign, this does not automatically make him a trespasser. The research states a trespasser is someone entering *without invitation*. Prad was a customer in the store with permission to be there, even if he briefly entered a restricted area. The element of lacking invitation is missing.
**(B) No, unless Prad's injuries resulted from the defendant's willful or wanton misconduct.** - This is incorrect. The duty owed is not limited to willful or wanton misconduct. The research establishes a duty of ordinary care to customers (invitees) to maintain a reasonably safe condition and warn of dangers. The element of a higher duty standard is missing.
**(C) Yes, because the premises were defective with respect to a public invitee.** - This is too broad. While Prad was likely an invitee, the mere existence of a defect (the open shaft) isn’t enough for liability. The store owner is not automatically liable for *any* defect. The element of knowledge of the defect and failure to warn or remedy is missing.
**(D) Yes, if the clerk had reason to believe that Prad was unaware of the open shaft.** - This is correct. The research establishes a duty to warn of *unknown* dangers. The clerk *knew* of the shaft but *assumed* Prad knew about it. If the clerk had no reasonable basis to believe Prad was aware, the store breached its duty of care. All elements are satisfied: duty of care to an invitee, knowledge of a hidden danger, and failure to warn. The comparative negligence rule doesn’t negate liability, it only reduces recovery.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (C)**
Reasoning:
(A) recover, because the skunk was a private nuisance: To establish a nuisance, Walker must prove (1) a dangerous condition, (2) a continuing danger, (3) unreasonable/unlawful use, and (4) proximate cause. While the skunk *created* a danger, Householder did not create the danger through an *unreasonable* use of land. He attempted to mitigate the risk by having the scent gland removed, even though the vet failed. This element is missing.
(B) recover, because the skunk was not a domesticated animal: The research does not establish a rule that the status of “domesticated” versus “not domesticated” is relevant to liability for injuries caused by animals on one’s property. This choice introduces a fact not supported by the legal research.
(C) not recover, because Walker was a trespasser: Householder owed Walker a duty to refrain from deliberately or maliciously causing injury as a trespasser. However, the facts do not indicate Householder *intended* to spray Walker with the skunk, nor did he act maliciously. The research states a landowner owes a trespasser a duty to refrain from deliberately or maliciously causing injury. Here, the spraying was an unintended consequence of the vet’s negligence. Walker was a trespasser, and Householder did not breach his limited duty of care. All elements are satisfied.
(D) not recover, because Dr. Vet was the cause of the injury: While Dr. Vet’s negligence was the *proximate* cause of the skunk retaining its scent, Householder put the skunk on his property knowing it *might* spray someone. The research does not address comparative or contributory negligence, so we cannot determine if Householder’s actions contributed to the injury. This choice focuses on another party’s negligence, not Householder’s liability.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (D)**
Reasoning:
**(A) constitutional, because it is reasonably related to the protection of the reputation of the fabric industry located in the state of Orange.** - This choice fails because the statute’s stated purpose is public safety, not protecting the reputation of the fabric industry. Even if reputation were incidentally benefitted, that doesn’t make the law constitutional if it violates the Commerce Clause. The rule requires examining whether the law discriminates against or unduly burdens interstate commerce. This choice doesn’t address that.

**(B) constitutional, because it is a legitimate means of protecting the safety of the public.** - While public safety is a legitimate state interest, the rule states that even legitimate interests must be pursued in a way that doesn’t violate the Commerce Clause. The statute *requires* testing by a specific, in-state company (Zetest). This potentially burdens interstate commerce by forcing companies like Fabric Mill to use a local tester, even if their usual out-of-state tester (Alpha) uses an identical process. The rule states a law is invalid if its burdens outweigh its benefits. This choice doesn’t analyze that.

**(C) unconstitutional, because it denies to Fabric Mill the equal protection of the laws.** - The facts concern a challenge under the Commerce Clause, not the Equal Protection Clause. The statute applies to all manufacturers in Orange, not just Fabric Mill, so there’s no denial of equal protection. The rule focuses on discrimination against *interstate* commerce, not discrimination between entities within a state.

**(D) unconstitutional, because it imposes an unreasonable burden on interstate commerce.** - This choice satisfies the rule. The statute requires all fabric manufacturers in Orange to use Zetest, a private, in-state testing company. Fabric Mill already uses Alpha, an out-of-state company that uses an identical testing process. This forces Fabric Mill to incur additional costs to use Zetest instead of Alpha, creating a burden on interstate commerce. The rule states a law violates the Commerce Clause if it imposes a burden on interstate commerce that outweighs any benefits received. While public safety is a benefit, forcing a company to switch to a local tester when an equally reliable out-of-state option exists suggests the burden outweighs the benefit. This choice best reflects the application of the dormant Commerce Clause.

  MC selection: **Answer: (B)**
Reasoning:
**(A)** is incorrect because the rule requires *performance* of the requested act (arrest and conviction) or detrimental reliance, not merely a promise to *attempt* the act. Gimlet didn’t accept by promising to try; he accepted by investigating and obtaining a confession leading to conviction. Element 1 (performance) is missing.
**(B)** is correct because Gimlet fully performed the acts requested by the offer: assisting in the arrest *and* the successful conviction of an arsonist. This satisfies Element 1 (performance of the requested act). The fact that Gimlet didn’t know about the revocation until *after* claiming the reward is irrelevant, as revocation is effective upon communication, not knowledge.
**(C)** is incorrect because merely *supplying information* is insufficient. The offer required the *arrest and conviction* of the arsonist. Supplying information is a step towards that, but doesn’t constitute full performance. Element 1 (full performance) is missing.
**(D)** is incorrect because the method of acceptance (performance) is not dictated by the method of the offer. The offer was made via broadcast, but acceptance occurs through performance of the requested act, not by repeating the broadcast. The rule does not require acceptance through the same medium as the offer. Element 1 (performance) is missing, and this choice introduces an irrelevant requirement.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (C)**
Reasoning:
(A) is incorrect because the rule states recklessness is *insufficient* to establish accomplice liability. Jack’s actions, while reprehensible, don’t meet the required mental state. He didn’t intend for Chip to be *shot* by a security guard, only that Chip would harm Morgan.
(B) is incorrect because transferred intent applies when the defendant intends to harm one victim but accidentally harms another. Here, Jack didn’t intend to harm anyone directly; he intended to incite Chip to harm Morgan. Transferred intent doesn’t apply to the security guard’s actions.
(C) is correct because Jack did not intend for Chip to be shot by the security guard. While Jack intended to incite Chip to violence against Morgan, the security guard’s action was an intervening act. The research indicates an accomplice is responsible for natural and probable consequences, but a shooting by a security guard attempting to prevent a crime is not a natural and probable consequence of inciting someone to commit an assault. Jack lacked the requisite intent for the *specific* crime of the security guard shooting Chip.
(D) is incorrect because Jack was not acting in concert with the security guard. The security guard’s actions were independent of Jack’s, and Jack did not intend for the security guard to shoot Chip. The research emphasizes the need for intent to aid or encourage the commission of a crime, and Jack’s intent was directed towards Chip harming Morgan, not a security guard shooting Chip.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (D)**
Reasoning:
(A) is incorrect because whether the police knew the item was cocaine *before* seizing it is irrelevant. The Fourth Amendment concerns the legality of the *seizure* itself, not the officer’s prior knowledge.
(B) is incorrect. The key question is whether the seizure was unlawful. Sandra discarded the bag of cocaine *after* the police told her to stop. The police did not seize the cocaine until *after* she discarded it. Abandonment of property is not an unlawful seizure.
(C) is incorrect. While the police may have ultimately developed probable cause when they retrieved and identified the cocaine, probable cause is not required for an initial investigatory detention (a *Terry* stop). The question is whether the initial stop and subsequent retrieval of the discarded item were lawful.
(D) is correct. Sandra abandoned the cocaine by throwing it into the bushes. The police were lawfully present where they retrieved the abandoned item. Abandonment of property eliminates a person’s reasonable expectation of privacy, meaning the police can seize it without a warrant or probable cause. The facts state Sandra stopped when told to do so and raised her hands, indicating she did not maintain control over the item once she discarded it. Therefore, the seizure was lawful.

The permits are effective for only one year from the date of their issuance and are awarded on the basis of a lottery, in which there is no differentiation between resident and nonresident applicants. However, each nonresident who obtains a permit is charged an annual permit fee that is $5 more than the fee charged residents. Fisher, Inc., is a large fishing company that operates from a port in another state and is incorporated in that other state. Each of the boats of Fisher, Inc., has a federal shipping license that permits it "to engage in all aspects of the coastal trade, to fish and to carry cargo from place to place along the coast, and to engage in other lawful activities along the coast of the United States." These shipping licenses are authorized by federal statute. Assume no other federal statutes or administrative rules apply. Although it had previously held an Atlantica oyster permit, Fisher, Inc., did not obtain a permit in that state's lottery this year. Which of the following is the strongest argument that can be made in support of a continued right of Fisher, Inc., to fish for oysters this year in the coastal waters of Atlantica?

Answer choices:
  (A) Because the Atlantica law provides higher permit charges for nonresidents, it is an undue burden on interstate commerce.
  (B) Because the Atlantica law provides higher permit charges for nonresidents, it denies Fisher, Inc., the privileges and immunities of state citizenship.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (A)**
Reasoning:
**(A) Because the Atlantica law provides higher permit charges for nonresidents, it is an undue burden on interstate commerce.** This choice directly addresses a potential violation of the dormant Commerce Clause. The rule states a state law violating the dormant Commerce Clause must be “narrowly tailored to advance a legitimate local purpose” if it discriminates against out-of-state economic actors. The facts state Atlantica charges nonresidents $5 more for permits, creating differential treatment based on residency. This is a discriminatory practice. While the state argues the law promotes conservation (a legitimate local purpose), the $5 fee difference is likely not narrowly tailored to achieve that purpose and constitutes an undue burden on interstate commerce. All elements of a dormant Commerce Clause violation are met.

**(B) Because the Atlantica law provides higher permit charges for nonresidents, it denies Fisher, Inc., the privileges and immunities of state citizenship.** The research on the Privileges and Immunities Clause requires the burdened activity to be “sufficiently basic to the livelihood of the Nation” and the restriction to be “closely related to the advancement of a substantial state interest.” The research states the evidence does not address whether a higher fee on out-of-state corporations engaging in a state’s fishing industry constitutes discrimination that substantially interferes with a fundamental right or important commercial activity. Therefore, this element is missing.

**(C) Because it holds a federal shipping license, Fisher, Inc., has a right to fish for oysters in Atlantica waters despite the state law.** The research does not support the idea that a federal shipping license automatically overrides state oyster fishing regulations. The federal license permits coastal trade, fishing, and cargo transport, but doesn’t negate the requirement for a state permit to fish in state waters. This choice assumes a right not supported by the research.

**(D) Because Fisher, Inc., previously held an Atlantica oyster permit and Atlantica knows that company is engaged in a continuing business operation, the refusal to grant Fisher, Inc., a permit this year is a taking of its property without due process of law.** The facts do not indicate a “taking” of property. Fisher, Inc. was not *guaranteed* a permit; it participated in a lottery and did not win. A prior permit does not create a vested property right to a future permit. The due process element is missing.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (D)**
Reasoning:
**(A)** Yes, because the higher crime rate in Foster's neighborhood demonstrates that the county police are not giving persons who reside there the equal protection of the laws. - This choice misapplies the Equal Protection Clause. The clause restricts *state* action, not the actions of private actors or local police departments in this context. The issue is whether the *insurance company’s* rate differential violates equal protection, and the police’s actions are irrelevant to that question. Missing element: State action.
**(B)** Yes, because the insurance rate differential is inherently discriminatory. - This choice asserts a conclusion without legal basis. A rate differential is not *inherently* discriminatory; it must be evaluated under the appropriate Equal Protection standard. The facts indicate an economic/social welfare classification (insurance rates), which requires a rational basis, not a showing of inherent discrimination. Missing element: Application of the correct standard of review.
**(C)** No, because the constitutional guarantee of equal protection of the laws is not applicable to the actions of these insurance companies. - This is incorrect. While the Equal Protection Clause primarily applies to state action, it *can* apply to private actors when there is sufficient state involvement. Here, the state insurance commissioner inspects companies for solvency, establishing some state involvement. However, this is not the deciding factor, as the rational basis test still applies. Missing element: Consideration of state involvement and the applicable standard of review.
**(D)** No, because there is a rational basis for the differential in insurance rates. - This choice correctly applies the rational basis test. The insurance company charges higher rates based on differing crime rates. A state has a legitimate governmental interest in ensuring financial stability and fair pricing within the insurance market. Charging rates commensurate with risk (higher rates in higher-crime areas) is rationally related to that interest. All elements are satisfied: economic/social welfare classification, legitimate governmental interest, rational relationship between the classification and the interest. No defense/exception applies.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (B)**
Reasoning:
The key issue is the distribution of the $200,000 surplus from the sale of the parking garage. The statute states the judgment lien attaches to real property *then owned or subsequently acquired* by the debtor. Lender One’s judgment lien attached to the parking garage when it was owned by Owner. Lender Two has a prior mortgage lien on the parking garage. Therefore, Lender Two is first entitled to the proceeds of the sale up to the amount of its mortgage. Any remaining surplus goes to the judgment lien holder, Lender One.

Let's analyze each choice:
(A) $100,000 to Lender One and $100,000 to Owner. - Incorrect. This does not prioritize Lender Two’s prior mortgage lien.
(B) $100,000 to Lender Two and $100,000 to Owner. - Correct. Lender Two’s mortgage is paid first up to its balance, and the remaining surplus goes to Owner.
(C) $100,000 to Lender One and $100,000 to Lender Two. - Incorrect. Lender Two’s mortgage has priority, and is paid before Lender One’s judgment lien.
(D) $200,000 to Owner. - Incorrect. This ignores Lender Two’s prior mortgage lien and Lender One’s judgment lien.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (A)**
Reasoning:
**(A) Jones, because Jones was a third-party creditor beneficiary of the contract between Sloan and Abstract Company** - This is the most likely correct answer. Abstract Company contracted with Sloan to provide an abstract of title to Jones. This creates a third-party beneficiary relationship. Abstract Company owed a duty of care to Jones to accurately prepare the abstract. The breach of that duty (omitting the right-of-way) caused Jones damages (the diminished value of the property). All elements of a negligence claim are met.

**(B) Jones, because the abstract prepared by Abstract Company constitutes a guarantee of Jones's title to Newacre** - The facts do not state that the abstract was a *guarantee* of title, only that it was an abstract. An abstract is an informational document, not a guarantee. This choice misstates the nature of the abstract.

**(C) Abstract Company, because Abstract Company had no knowledge of the existence of the right-of-way** - Lack of knowledge is not a defense to negligence. Abstract Company had a duty to exercise reasonable care in preparing the abstract, which includes searching public records. Failure to discover a recorded deed does not absolve them of liability.

**(D) Abstract Company, because there was no showing that any fraud was practiced upon Jones** - The claim is based on negligence, not fraud. Fraud requires intentional misrepresentation, which is not alleged here. The absence of fraud is irrelevant to a negligence claim.

The question asks about suppressing the *marijuana* as evidence, not the initial observation. Element: Warrant obtained based on observation - not met.
(D) is correct because the research states that there is no reasonable expectation of privacy from aerial observation [Source 3]. Officer Boa observed the marijuana from public airspace, satisfying the legal standard. Element: Aerial observation from public airspace - met. No defense or exception applies.

(A) is incorrect because while Pike has an opportunity to deny or explain, the research doesn’t state opportunity to explain *alone* makes evidence admissible. The probative value must outweigh the danger of unfair prejudice, confusion, or waste of time, which is not met here. The prior lawsuit is 30 years old and concerns a different house; its relevance to Pike’s current claim is weak.
(B) is incorrect because Pike’s failure to object to the initial question doesn’t automatically make Wirth’s testimony admissible. Evidence must still comply with evidentiary rules. The question itself may have been improper, and the answer doesn’t cure that.
(C) is incorrect because the best evidence rule applies to the *content* of a writing, recording, or photograph. Here, the testimony concerns the *fact* of a prior lawsuit and its settlement, not the contents of any document.
(D) is correct because the testimony’s probative value is substantially outweighed by the danger of confusion and wasted time. The lawsuit occurred 30 years ago, involving a different house and contractor. It’s unlikely to prove a strong motive or pattern of behavior. Introducing this evidence risks confusing the jury with irrelevant information and wasting time on a tangential issue. The research supports the idea that even if relevant, evidence can be excluded if its probative value is outweighed by other dangers.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (B)**
Reasoning:
**(A)** Yes, because Walker was injured by an artificial condition of the premises while using an adjacent public way.
- This choice *could* be correct, but the research doesn’t establish a specific rule about “artificial conditions” or adjacent public ways. The research only states landowners owe a duty of care to make premises reasonably safe for invitees and can be liable for created or known hazards. It doesn’t define what constitutes an “artificial condition” sufficient to trigger liability.

**(B)** Yes, because such an accident does not ordinarily happen in the absence of negligence.
- This choice aligns with the principle of *res ipsa loquitur*, which isn’t explicitly stated in the research but is a logical extension of the duty to maintain safe premises. The falling flowerpot is an event that doesn’t typically occur without negligence. The research establishes landowners have a duty to inspect and repair, implying negligence if a hazard causes injury. All elements are satisfied: the accident is of a type that ordinarily doesn’t happen without negligence, the instrumentality causing the injury (flowerpot) was under the defendant’s exclusive control, and the plaintiff’s injury wasn’t due to their own actions.

**(C)** No, if Landco is in no better position than Walker to explain the accident.
- This choice describes a situation where *res ipsa loquitur* would *not* apply. However, the research doesn’t focus on the application of *res ipsa loquitur* or the relative ability of parties to explain an accident. It focuses on the duty of care and liability for hazards.

**(D)** No, because there is no basis for a reasonable inference that Landco was negligent.
- This choice is incorrect. The falling flowerpot *does* create a basis for a reasonable inference of negligence. The research states landowners have a duty to inspect and repair to avoid injuries. A flowerpot falling suggests a failure in inspection or maintenance, thus a breach of duty. The research supports a reasonable inference of negligence.

--- VERIFY / MC SELECT NODE ---
  MC choices detected — selecting answer...
  MC selection: **Answer: (C)**
Reasoning:
**(A) Edward, because Dennis has no rights in the stairway.** This is incorrect. An easement by implication likely exists. Simon, as common owner, created an obvious and permanent servitude (the stairway) when both buildings were under his ownership. This servitude was reasonably necessary for the enjoyment of Building 2 (access to the second floor). Therefore, Dennis, as a subsequent owner of Building 2, likely has an easement by implication.

**(B) Edward, because Dennis's rights in the stairway do not extend beyond the normal life of the existing structure.** The research does not support a limitation on the duration of an easement by implication based on the structure’s lifespan. The easement exists as long as the necessity continues.

**(C) Dennis, because Dennis has an easement in the stairway and an implied right to keep the stairway in repair.** This is correct. As explained above, an easement by implication likely exists. While the research doesn’t *explicitly* state a right to repair, it is reasonably implied. An easement holder has the right to do what is reasonably necessary to *use* the easement, and a structurally unsound stairway renders the easement unusable. Therefore, Dennis has an implied right to repair the stairway to maintain access.

**(D) Dennis, because Dennis has a right to take whatever action is necessary to protect himself from possible tort liability to persons using the stairway.** While Dennis may have a duty to avoid creating a dangerous condition, this doesn’t automatically grant him the right to enter Edward’s property to make repairs. The right stems from the easement itself, not solely from potential tort liability. This choice attempts to create a justification where the easement provides the primary right.

  MC selection: **Answer: (A)**
Reasoning:
**(A) No, because a state must comply with valid federal laws that regulate matters affecting interstate commerce.** This choice correctly applies the Supremacy Clause. The federal law regulates tire disposal, which inherently affects interstate commerce (tires are manufactured and transported across state lines). East Dakota is violating a valid federal law by using an unlicensed facility, regardless of whether the facility *almost* meets federal standards. Element 1 (valid federal law) is met. Element 2 (regulation of interstate commerce) is met. There is a direct conflict – the state is required to use licensed facilities, but is using an unlicensed one.
**(B) No, because some of the tires come from vehicles that are used by the state solely in its commercial activities.** This choice focuses on *some* tires, but the law applies to *all* tires discarded in the country. The source material does not suggest a distinction based on the source of the tires (commercial vs. non-commercial). Missing element: universal application of the law.
**(C) Yes, because some of the tires come from vehicles that are used by the state in the performance of core state governmental functions such as law enforcement.** This choice attempts to invoke a state sovereignty exception, but the research does not support such an exception to the Supremacy Clause. The fact that some tires come from police cars does not exempt the state from complying with the federal law. Missing element: a valid exception to the Supremacy Clause.
**(D) Yes, because the legitimate needs of the federal government are satisfied by the fact that the unlicensed state disposal scheme meets, in actual practice, most of the federal standards for the licensing of such facilities.** This choice suggests that substantial compliance is sufficient. However, the federal law *requires* licensing, not just substantial compliance. The research establishes that conflicting state law must give way to federal law, and “most” standards being met does not negate the conflict. Missing element: full compliance with the federal law’s licensing requirement.

(A) is incorrect: Walking across the courtroom does not involve compelled *testimony* that could be self-incriminating. The act of walking itself doesn’t reveal any information about guilt or provide a lead to evidence of a crime. The Fifth Amendment protects against compelled statements, not physical acts. Missing element: Incriminating testimony.
(B) is incorrect: Requiring Devlin to walk does not constitute a search or seizure. It’s a physical demonstration of an existing characteristic, not an intrusion into a protected area or the taking of a physical item. Missing element: No search or seizure occurred.
(C) is correct: The request to walk is a legitimate method to assist the jury in identifying Devlin based on a witness description (the limp). The research indicates that identification procedures are permissible unless *unnecessarily suggestive*. Simply demonstrating a pre-existing physical characteristic isn’t inherently suggestive. All elements are satisfied: It’s a valid identification procedure.
(D) is incorrect: While defendants don’t have absolute privacy rights, this isn’t a privacy issue. The request concerns a physical characteristic already observable, and the question is about the admissibility of evidence, not a violation of privacy. Missing element: This is not a privacy concern.
